class="text-primary uppercase" data-tealium-class="o01__article-topic-link">Fraud</a></div><h1 data-testid="text-treatment-tackling-gootkit-s-traps-article-hero-heading" class="d-h1 text-grayscale-200">Tackling Gootkit&#x27;s Traps</h1><h5 data-testid="text-treatment-f5-article-hero-sub-headline" class="d-body-lead text-grayscale-200">Gootkit malware uses misleading code to hinder manual research and automated analysis.</h5><div data-testid="test-id-for-f5-grid" class="grid grid-cols-1 gap-4 text-grayscale-200 d-body-sm !font-semibold"><p><p>By <a href="/labs/search/#q=Julia Karpin" data-tealium-class="o01__author-name tag-item">Julia<!-- --> <!-- -->Karpin</a> </p></p><p><span class="publish-date">7/11/2018</span><span class="content-duration">6 min. read</span></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  pt-48 lg:pt-64 pb-48 lg:pb-64  bg-grayscale"><div data-testid="test-id-for-wrapper" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent container"><div class="article-layout"><div id="article-content" class="art-layout-content"><div id="f5-article-content-section_tackling-gootkit-s-traps"                   }
              }</script><div id="article-table-of-contents" data-testid="test-id-for-article-table-of-contents" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent article-toc hide-below-lg"><div class="collapse collapse-arrow collapse-close f5-accordion"><input data-testid="test-id-for-undefined" type="radio" name="accordion"/><div class="collapse-title f5-accordion-title text-lg">Table of Contents</div><div class="collapse-content f5-accordion-content"><nav><ul class="article-toc-list"><li><a href="#first_glance" data-item-id="first_glance" class="d-body-lead" data-tealium-class="cont03-toc-item__title-link">First Glance</a></li><li><a href="#diving_deeper_into_the_assembly_code" data-item-id="diving_deeper_into_the_assembly_code" class="d-body-lead" data-tealium-class="cont03-toc-item__title-link">Diving Deeper into the Assembly Code</a></li><li><a href="#following_the_traps" data-item-id="following_the_traps" class="d-body-lead" data-tealium-class="cont03-toc-item__title-link">Following the Traps</a></li><li><a href="#the_road_not_taken" data-item-id="the_road_not_taken" class="d-body-lead" data-tealium-class="cont03-toc-item__title-link">The Road Not Taken</a></li><li><a href="#conclusion" data-item-id="conclusion" class="d-body-lead" data-tealium-class="cont03-toc-item__title-link">Conclusion</a></li><li><a href="#appendix" data-item-id="appendix" class="d-body-lead" data-tealium-class="cont03-toc-item__title-link">Appendix</a></li></ul></nav></div></div></div><div class="f5-text text-grayscale-700" data-testid="custom-portable-text-rich-text-wrapper"><div class="prose lg:prose-lg max-w-none f5-text-rte lg:prose-p:leading-article-content" data-testid="custom-portable-text-rich-text-richtext-wrapper"><p class="article-content-paragraph">Gootkit is an advanced banking trojan first discovered in mid-2014. Known for using various techniques to evade detection, the malware also has its own unique twists: it’s partially written in JavaScript and it incorporates the node.js runtime environment.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Gootkit employs several checks in order to identify a virtual environment and halt its propagation sequence once that happens. In this post, we’ll demonstrate a way to tackle these anti-research methods in order to have the sample executing in a virtual environment.</p><h2 id="first_glance" class="article-content-header-2">First Glance</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">Running the sample in a virtual machine results in the creation of several threads, but no modifications to the system are made. Without interruptions, Gootkit’s process would continue to run forever.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Running the sample in a debugger allows us to interrupt the process and inspect its execution flow. Whenever the debugger is paused, Gootkit’s process is sleeping.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">According to the return address, the call comes from the malware (not from a system library) and the parameter is -1, meaning: sleep forever.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">How can we make the malware go to a more interesting execution flow in the code?</p><h2 id="diving_deeper_into_the_assembly_code" class="article-content-header-2">Diving Deeper into the Assembly Code</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">Looking at the assembly of the function that calls the sleep API, there are three possible execution paths: #1 and #2 are paths that we could force the malware to take by patching the conditional jumps that lead to them; #3 is the path it takes before reaching the sleep API.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">There are 3 possible paths of execution. We’ll use a shortcut in order to decide quickly which one is the most interesting one to follow. That shortcut involves checking cross-references(xrefs) from each function, which will indicate where most of the activity would take place.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Execution path #1, contains a call to sub_4047EC, which had no xrefs.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Execution path #2, contains a call to sub_409450, which had just a few xrefs.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Execution path #3 creates a thread that will execute sub_40C8C3, which had many xrefs.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">So, execution path #3, the newly created thread, seems like the one to take because it has a massive function graph, indicating that all the activity will most likely happen there.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">After placing a breakpoint at the beginning of the thread function(0x40C8C3), we start stepping through. One of the subroutines gets stuck in an endless loop, which means Gootkit is sleeping again!</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Recap #1: There are two threads, one of which is sleeping forever, but it is not the interesting one. The second one is in a sleep loop, and our goal is to force it to take a different branch in order to execute the interesting functionality.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">The only way to break the loop is to make the string comparison(StrStrIW) fail. The comparison parameters:</p><ul class="list-none"><li class="my-5 flex custom-bullet-item"><div id="custom-bullet-item-icon-fdc70b13-9ecd-41b5-b7dd-6ee910050ac3" data-testid="test-id-for-custom-bullet-item-icon-fdc70b13-9ecd-41b5-b7dd-6ee910050ac3" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent">Hard-coded string “Xeon”</div></li><li class="my-5 flex custom-bullet-item"><div 0.9L12.1 2.4L15 4ZM13.8 4.7L9.8 2.4L8 1.3L6.2 2.3L2.2 4.7V11.3L8 14.7L13.8 11.4V4.7Z" fill="#E6E6E6"></path></g><defs><clipPath id="clip0_646_27469"><rect width="16" height="16" fill="white"></rect></clipPath></defs></svg></div></div><div id="custom-bullet-item-data-bc807843-75ba-48ef-b060-33ba233e12e8" data-testid="test-id-for-custom-bullet-item-data-bc807843-75ba-48ef-b060-33ba233e12e8" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent">Data from <em>HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\HARDWARE\DESCRIPTION\System\CentralProcessor\0\ProcessorNameString</em></div></li></ul><p class="article-content-paragraph">If we changed this value in the registry, we could successfully overcome this hurdle. But, would that be the end? Would it run? Sadly, after making this change, Gootkit goes back to sleep in another endless loop, this time in a different subroutine.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Recap #2: Again, there are two threads, one that is sleeping forever (but not interesting); the other is running in a sleep loop.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">After inspecting the condition that leads us to the sleep API call, it becomes clear that this branch is taken when the “GetProcAddress” fails. But, why would this simple API fail?</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">At second glance, it is evident that the first parameter of the “GetProcAddress” API, the HModule, is always equal to null because of the XOR instruction highlighted in red. The conclusion is that this API is meant to fail! This whole function is a trap, and our goal is to avoid entering it altogether.</p><h2 id="following_the_traps" class="article-content-header-2">Following the Traps</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">We’ll rename the function to “trap” and then check all the function references to see how many traps are there.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">There are 17 traps to overcome and they are all in the same function, so we assume this is the function making all the environment checks. We’ll have to check each condition that leads to a trap and make sure to change the environment (or patch the binary) in a way that would bypass the trap.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Figure 8 shows a code snippet from the sub_409AE2 function.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Note that each “while” loop is performing string decryption on the sequences of bytes shown in the variables above the loop. When following the execution in a debugger, the strings are decrypted, and some meaningful indicators of VM checks are visible. (See appendix for decryption function details.)</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">In this code snippet, three checks are evident:</p><ul class="list-none"><li class="my-5 flex custom-bullet-item"><div id="custom-bullet-item-icon-82b93489-2725-4ee1-931e-00ec4be74ccc" data-testid="test-id-for-custom-bullet-item-icon-82b93489-2725-4ee1-931e-00ec4be74ccc" class="f5-section 0.9L12.1 2.4L15 4ZM13.8 4.7L9.8 2.4L8 1.3L6.2 2.3L2.2 4.7V11.3L8 14.7L13.8 11.4V4.7Z" fill="#E6E6E6"></path></g><defs><clipPath id="clip0_646_27469"><rect width="16" height="16" address check</div></li><li class="my-5 flex custom-bullet-item"><div id="custom-bullet-item-icon-bcddc2fa-f766-442b-91f1-a249cd20ad63" xl:bg-transparent">Checking the presence of “sbiedll.dll” — sandboxie indicator</div></li></ul><p class="article-content-paragraph">By following the traps and patching the system accordingly, the 0.9L12.1 2.4L15 4ZM13.8 4.7L9.8 2.4L8 1.3L6.2 2.3L2.2 4.7V11.3L8 14.7L13.8 11.4V4.7Z" fill="#E6E6E6"></path></g><defs><clipPath id="clip0_646_27469"><rect width="16" height="16" fill="white"></rect></clipPath></defs></svg></div></div><div id="custom-bullet-item-data-1a3273f5-f8c0-42f0-a36c-b72c3b6d5608" data-testid="test-id-for-custom-bullet-item-data-1a3273f5-f8c0-42f0-a36c-b72c3b6d5608" class="f5-section  bg-transparent sm:bg-transparent md:bg-transparent lg:bg-transparent xl:bg-transparent"><em>HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\HARDWARE\DESCRIPTION\System\SystemBiosVersion&quot; </em>compare with AMI, VirtualBox, BOCHS, INTEL 640000, 55274-640-2673064-23950, and other serials</div></li></ul><p class="article-content-paragraph">After patching a virtual machine and running the sample, it’s clear that it is no longer stuck in an endless loop and that the sample continues its propagation in the system.</p><h2 id="the_road_not_taken" class="article-content-header-2">The Road Not Taken</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">Patching the virtual environment by changing the MAC address and several registry values is one way to get the sample to run. It is the recommended way to go when running Gootkit samples in an automated analysis system. However, this post would not be complete without presenting the other solution: patching the binary itself.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">Every time there’s a conditional jump to the trap function, one could change it to a simple jump opcode, thus skipping the trap function. This would direct the flow from the jump to the next code block while skipping the trap function.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">However, this patch must be applied 17 times, and that’s a tedious task. Instead, since all the calls to the trap function come from one function, its preferable to patch and bypass the call to that function.</p><h2 id="conclusion" class="article-content-header-2">Conclusion</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">When analyzing malware, especially intricate, multi-threaded ones like Gootkit, it is important to choose the interesting thread to follow and to keep in mind that even functions that look like they contain seemingly “ordinary” code may be used as a deception.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">MD5: 47f8d6d3e4410218c4c64701cb7d7d3d</p><h2 id="appendix" class="article-content-header-2">Appendix</h2><p class="article-content-paragraph">The decryption function is an XOR loop with a hard-coded key. The encrypted string and the key are hard-coded sequences of bytes.</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">In Python:</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">An example of the string decryption loop and its parameters:</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">encrypted_string = &quot;\x3A\x03\x05\x15\x55\x0E\x25\x4F\x08\x1C\x5D\x62&quot;</p><p class="article-content-paragraph">key = &quot;\x49\x61\x6C\x70\x31\x62&quot;</p></div></div><!--$--><!--/$--><div id="authors-layout" data-testid="testid-for-authors-layout" class="grid grid-cols-1 gap-32"><div id="authors-heading" Protection is the range of security options that defenders use to protect apps and their related data from a range of threats. This includes process-based practices such as securing applications during development (DevSecOps), access controls, code obfuscation, and patch management. It also includes active protection in the form of encryption, anomaly detection, and firewalls. The goal is to prevent unauthorized access, data breaches, tampering, or exploitation of vulnerabilities that could compromise the integrity and functionality of the applications.\n\nThis stems from a recognition that apps are increasingly the target of choice for malicious actors, for much the same reason that criminals rob banks: that's \"where the money is\". Given that, it increasingly makes sense for defenders to be app-centric in their protection models and mitigation strategies. Providing robust application security ensures the safety of sensitive information, builds customer trust, ensures regulatory compliance, and minimizes financial and reputational damages.1f:T548,Threat actors target applications by exploiting vulnerabilities and misconfigurations. Common attack methods include injecting malicious code through techniques like SQL injection, cross-site scripting (XSS), and cross-site request forgery (CSRF), as well as abusing APIs to access sensitive data. They also exploit insec"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"ure authentication by using stolen credentials (via credential stuffing) or brute force attacks, and conduct malware injections, supply chain attacks, and reverse engineering to compromise the application or its users. Threat actors may also take advantage of misconfigured environments, such as exposed admin dashboards, unencrypted data, or default credentials. Denial-of-service (DoS) attacks can overwhelm applications and degrade their availability, while zero-day exploits leverage undiscovered vulnerabilities before they are patched.\n\nIn addition to technical attacks, threat actors use social engineering tactics, such as phishing, to manipulate users into revealing credentials or performing unauthorized actions. They may also target client-side vulnerabilities, such as intercepting unencrypted traffic or injecting malicious scripts into websites via formjacking. These attacks are often automated using tools to scan for application weaknesses or abuse APIs, but can also involve highly targeted, manual exploitation."])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"19:[\"$\",\"footer\",null,{\"children\":[[\"$\",\"div\",null,{\"id\":\"footer-credibility-section\",\"data-testid\":\"test-id-for-footer-credibility-section\",\"style\":{},\"className\":\"f5-section Labs, threat research is a broad endeavor. It includes analyzing vulnerabilities and their impacts, attacker trend data, malware investigation, development of open source tools to identify attacks and mitigate exposure, industry and regional assessments, and consideration of a wide range of attacker tactics and techniques. The attack surface is always expanding, which means attackers are always finding new vulnerabilities to exploit and defenders are always having to develop new ways to stop them.\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"normal\"}],\"designHint\":\"card-right\"},{\"_key\":\"ba1c4e893960\",\"_type\":\"labsTextAndArticleCardsBlock\",\"articles\":[{\"_key\":\"b2d47c4272dc\",\"_ref\":\"6e6dce4d-b97e-4116-b500-0eaafd371c65\",\"_type\":\"reference\"}],\"content\":[{\"_key\":\"1acc3e5ab034\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"758546c50687\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"What Are the Risks You Need to Worry About?\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"h2\"},{\"_key\":\"d47be32b7822\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"6d87f224d0e2\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"This is a complex question. Beneficial bots support search engines, fulfill genuine business needs, and automate repetitive tasks. While some are outright malicious and enable fraud, many other bots operate somewhere in the middle—in the murky grey area between illegal and immoral. They ignore website terms of use, prevent customers from purchasing goods and services, and can even weaken security. Existing research often examines uncontrolled bot traffic across the entire internet, but this report takes a different approach and, instead, focuses on how automated traffic changes after bot mitigation is put in place.\"}],\"markDefs\":[],\"style\":\"normal\"},{\"_key\":\"39521a04bb40\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"76f2ee6c40ac\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"We provide a detailed analysis of bot types, their objectives, and industry-specific impacts. A key focus is on advanced and persistent bots—those that adapt as security measures evolve. Malicious actors, including nation-states and cybercriminals, frequently modify tactics to bypass defenses, increasing attack sophistication or moving to softer targets. Furthermore, over time threat actors have found reliable methods for \"},{\"_key\":\"27bd34cf86f4\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[\"strong\",\"eaa0d7e19ecd\"],\"text\":\"bypassing CAPTCHA\"},{\"_key\":\"230089387600\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\", so even if this degree of user friction is acceptable, it is no longer effective.\"}],\"markDefs\":[{\"_key\":\"eaa0d7e19ecd\",\"_type\":\"internalLink\",\"reference\":{\"_ref\":\"labs-article-import-5859aa1e-572f-4ec2-bed0-f09f5e6d4d76\",\"_type\":\"reference\"}}],\"style\":\"normal\"},{\"_key\":\"65c72cac73a0\",\"_type\":\"block\",\"children\":[{\"_key\":\"cd41a26d8fc6\",\"_type\":\"span\",\"marks\":[],\"text\":\"As attackers adapt to older bot mitigation strategies, organizations are increasingly turning to tools that detect bot traffic based on a combination of behavioral and technical signals.
